# SOLIDWORKS 2022 Essential Training

> SOLIDWORKS is the premier product-design software—enabling you to deliver innovative solutions to market faster. In this course, Gabriel Corbett teaches you how to build ready-to-manufacture parts and assemblies as well as detailed drawings and itemized bills of materials in SOLIDWORKS 2022. Learn how to create 2D sketches and then extrude, revolve, loft, and sweep these features into 3D objects. 

> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2022-essential-training) | 8h 54m | 1K learners
> [Jump to Path Context ↓](#path-context)

### Introduction

[↑ Back to Table of Contents](#table-of-contents)

#### [Learn 3D CAD modeling using SOLIDWORKS 2022](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2022-essential-training/learn-3d-cad-modeling-using-solidworks-2022?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2022-essential-training/learn-3d-cad-modeling-using-solidworks-2022?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Gabriel] Hi, I'm Gabriel Corbett, and welcome to [[SOLIDWORKS]] 2022 Essential Training. In my [[LinkedIn]] Learning course, you'll get up to speed on creating complex parts, assemblies and drawings. We'll cover all the key tools and techniques so you can focus on your design and not the software. We'll start with the basic sketch tools and create two-dimensional sketches that will become the foundation for 3D objects. Next, we'll look at modeling 3D features by extruding or evolving sketches into 3D parts. Followed by creating more complex objects using the sweep, loft, and surface tools. Parts will be modified using a suite of tools that includes Fillets, Chamfers, Ribs, Draft, Shell, plus so much more. And that's just the beginning. We will cover the best practices for putting parts together into assemblies and building robust structures. Finally, we'll see how to create drawings to relate the final parts in assemblies to a manufacturer, complete with an itemized bill of materials and drawing notes. This course is for anyone looking to get into the world of 3D design and will benefit new users as well as those transitioning from other software packages. Are you ready to upgrade your skills and learn one of the most powerful 3D CAD design software packages in the industry? Join me on LinkedIn Learning for SOLIDWORKS 2022 Essential Training.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Frameworks & Libraries:** [[SOLIDWORKS]] (2), [[LinkedIn]] (2)
> **Env Vars:** solidworks (2), cad (1)
> **Speakers:** - [gabriel] (1)

#### [Launching SOLIDWORKS for the first time](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2022-essential-training/launching-solidworks-for-the-first-time?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2022-essential-training/launching-solidworks-for-the-first-time?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] [[SOLIDWORKS]] is the world leader in 3D solid modeling software. SolidWorks is both a history and feature-based parametric modeling program. The software uses a simple set of tools and commands to help you design parts, assemblies and drawings. On your screen you should see an icon that looks like this, double click on that and that should fire up SolidWorks. Give that a second to load. You may see a splash screen at this point in time, if not that's okay. And then the SolidWorks program or interface should load up here in a second. And we can start looking at the individual components that make up the software. Now there's actually three different modes inside of SolidWorks. The first one is the part mode, we have the assembly mode as well as the drawing mode. So this is the kind of the little home screen here, If you can click on this little home icon here, that'll bring that up. This allows us to start a brand new part or take a look at some of the recent documents we have down here. We can take a look at some of the recent folders and as well as some other resources like what's new in my SolidWorks and things like that. And notice that I am signed in right up here and you can sign into your account and have access to some of the resources there as well. You also have tabs for recent learn and alerts up there. And notice these are the basic parts. And so everything inside of SolidWorks starts with a template. So there's a basic template that loads with SolidWorks but you can create your own templates that is defined for maybe the company you work for or specific presets. So like if it's in-the-inch system or maybe the metric system. So you can set up as many templates as you'd like for either the part, assembly or drawing environments. But if you just click on this part environment here it just fires up the default template

Next you have this little welcome screen or I call it the home icon, which we just looked at. You've got the new document. You've got the open document, save, print and so on. > **[3:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2022-essential-training/launching-solidworks-for-the-first-time?u=76281980&t=186)** You can get a brand new tab called surfaces and you've got all the tools inside of there. If you've got one you don't want to see, right click on it, go back to tabs and go ahead and just turn that right off. You can still find 'em from up here or search form up here under search commands, but sometimes it's nice to have a nice little icon. Now over here on the left hand side of the screen is the feature manager. That is kind of the bin where we're going to keep everything that builds our model, right? So I told you, this is a history based modeler. So as you build features they're going to happen one after another. And you can roll the history bar back to go back in time and see how that model is created and it's all going to start here. So you have three fundamental planes in SolidWorks you have the front plane, the top plane and the right plane and everything in SolidWorks has to start on one of those planes. So you have to start a sketch, draw some shape out let's say it's a rectangle and then you're going to extrude that or revolve that or sweep that to turn that into a solid. And once you have that solid, now we're going to start either on one of those three fundamental planes or one of the planes of the part you just created. So if you have a block, I can choose any of the faces on the block, or I can go back to one of these planes. And again these planes are the fundamental planes

#### [Navigating in the 3D workspace](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2022-essential-training/navigating-in-the-3d-workspace?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2022-essential-training/navigating-in-the-3d-workspace?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] In this movie, we're going to focus on navigating in the 3D environment. So the first thing I want to do is open up a file. So I'm going to go up to the top of the screen, click on open and notice I've got Desktop, Exercise Files, Chapter one and then I'm going to open file 1.2, go ahead and click on open. All right. So the basics, if I want to row rotate this model around you need to have a three button mouse. Now if you hold down the middle mouse button or the scroll wheel so you can actually push down on that scroll wheel that'll allow you to rotate the model right and left and up and down. Now if you actually scroll that will [[Zoom]] in and zoom out. And then if you hold down Shift on your keyboard what that'll is also zoom in and zoom out or you can hold down Control and then push down that middle mouse button and that'll pan your model back and forth or up and down. All right. So those are your basics for just moving your model around again, push down on the middle mouse button which will give you rotate, zoom in zoom out just by scrolling or holding down Shift and then Control will pan. All right? Now at the top of the screen, you've got zoom to fit click on that. It'll just bring the model 100% into the window. Now if your model is somewhere way out there hit that button and it'll bring it in and same thing if it's way out there or somewhere you can't find it. If you hit F on the keyboard that'll also bring that or zoom to fit and notice if you look on the little drop down here it says zoom to fit and then it says a little F that is the fit. Next one is going to be Zoom to Area. Click on that and go ahead and drag a little box around what you'd like to look at.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2022-essential-training/navigating-in-the-3d-workspace?u=76281980&t=93)** Let go and it's going to zoom in to that area. Now if you want to go back to the previous view, go ahead and click on this next icon here which is Previous View and it takes you back to where you were. The next one is Section View. A Section View is going to allow you to use a section plane to cut through your model to look at the inside of it. Now I can grab that and I can slide it up through the part or I can switch to a different plane like this one over here might be the top plane and then I can just drag that through my model and see what's going on inside there. Same thing with the right plane, drag it through or you can even grab one of these and rotate it around. So a bunch of things you can do there. When you're done click on okay and it's going to cut that model in half but this is just a visual representation. It's not the real model, right? I'm just looking at it sliced in half but if you turn that feature off your whole model comes back. Next one is going to be the View Orientation. If you click on the dropdown it gives you this heads-up display called the view cube and that has a whole bunch of options of how you might want to look at the model choose any one of those faces and it's going to spin around so you're looking directly at it. Now there's a whole bunch other views you can choose directly from this menu here but they're all pretty much the same views here which you might find down here on the view cube. All right, let's pick one of those. Next one is going to be the Display Style. >

#### [Saving, renaming, and managing files](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2022-essential-training/saving-renaming-and-managing-files?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2022-essential-training/saving-renaming-and-managing-files?u=76281980&t=0)** - (Narrator) In this movie, let's talk about the different file types inside of [[SOLIDWORKS]] and how to save and modify those parts. Now, the basic structure for any type of SolidWorks assembly is you've got one kind of top level assembly. And below that you might have maybe a subassembly or multiple sub assemblies, which are made up of individual parts. And you can name them all however you'd like, and each one of those parts might have its own drawing. And the assembly itself might have its own drawing as well. And all of those different drawings and parts are looking for a path back to that assembly. So when you open an assembly it's looking for those parts, and the drawing is looking for that part as well. So they're all linked together. So if you just took one of those files and you moved it somewhere else and you tried to open it, for instance, the assembly you could open the assembly, but it'd say, "Hey, where are these parts that I'm looking for?" Cuz it's looking for the exact path of where it was. So it's always a good idea to kind of keep all those parts, assemblies and drawings together in the same file structure. The same thing happens if you were to change the name. So if you were to change the name of one of these parts, SolidWorks wouldn't know where to find it. Let me show you how that works. Let's head over here to SolidWorks. I'm going to go ahead and open up an assembly called 1.4. So that's under exercise files, chapter one, 1.4 Open up the assembly. And this is a basic assembly made up of three parts. So I have part one, two and three. So it's 1.4 dash one, two and three. All right. Pretty straightforward and simple.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2022-essential-training/saving-renaming-and-managing-files?u=76281980&t=93)** Now let's go ahead and close out of that, right. So pretty simple and straightforward to do that in [[Windows]] Explorer, right? It's no problem right? Now I've changed the name. Now let's go back over to SolidWorks and see what happens. So I'm here now in SolidWorks. I'm going to go ahead and open that exact same thing. So I need a browse now for that file. And I could say, "Hey, now it's actually called 1.4 dash four," but that is not really that great of a thing to do. And if you're only looking for one file it's not that big a deal, but what if you're looking for hundreds of files or maybe even thousands of files? That can cause a lot of issues. So I definitely recommend don't use Windows Explorer to just wildly change names on a whim. Make sure you think it through, name the files correctly the first time and then don't change 'em and don't change the locations if you can help it. I'd also recommend using a program like Treehouse which ships with SolidWorks, or even just [[Microsoft Excel|Excel]]. Let's head back over here to SolidWorks. Okay. So I want to cancel out of this. I don't want to make these changes. I'm going to go ahead and close this, close out of this. Don't save it, right. Go back over here to our file system. Change this back to a different name, dash three. And now if we go back to SolidWorks everything should be just fine. But now for SolidWorks 2020 and beyond we have a cool new tool that's been added. Now we used to have this add-in that was called SolidWorks Explorer, not Windows Explorer but SolidWorks Explorer used to be the way we could change these files. And what it would do, it would look through the file system and search to see if there's any references and then update those references. Now for SolidWorks 2020, that's been updated, and it's actually right inside of Windows Explorer. So if you actually right click on this, notice we get this little icon here that says SolidWorks. And over here, we have the option to open the file, do a pack and go, we can rename the file, replace it, move it, and look for a file location. So it's all inside of Windows Explorer, but you have to make sure you're using or loading in this package when you install SolidWorks otherwise you won't see it. So make sure you're looking for the file utilities program that's included with SolidWorks to get this functionality and then you can come over here and say rename and go in here, retype the new name. It's going to look for where it's being used

So if you want to go in and change the shape of your part, go back to that sketch that defined it, modify it drag it out here or something like that, something wild, then exit out of that sketch and notice the part automatically updates, right? Did this part change? No, because that is a separate feature that uses a separate sketch. If you want to change that feature or that sketch, go ahead and change either the feature itself, which defines how long it is or the sketch, which shows what shape it is. > **[6:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2022-essential-training/creating-your-first-3d-part?u=76281980&t=372)** So I'm going to cut that into the part. Click OK and now I've cut a hole or a bunch holes. Pretty nice. So anyways, those are the basics for creating parts inside of SolidWorks. Again, to recap you choose a face or a plane, you draw a sketch. It generally needs to be an enclosed boundary and then either extrude it or if you already have a shape, you can cut into that shape and then just build one feature at a time to create your final shape.

And right there, I've only selected just the part that was common to both of those pieces. And I selected that cut. So you can see there's a whole bunch different ways that SolidWorks interprets sketches. And if it doesn't have enough information it's going to ask you for the right information. Or it might even just fail if you just give it like a line, or it might try to create a thin feature you don't ever know. I mean, so there's a lot of things going on there but just keep in mind the way that the interaction happens between a feature and a sketch. Now, the sketch it's always going to be a generally enclosed boundary or multiple enclosed boundaries that you need to have. And then that's going to define that shape. And then the extrude or the cut will define the length of how far that comes out. That's the basics for [[Sketching]] in SolidWorks.

You can have as much or as little in your original sketch as you'd like. And then just define how far you'd like to cut around in your shape. And that's what you need to know to use the revolved cut feature inside of Solid Works.

#### [Finding mass properties](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2022-essential-training/finding-mass-properties?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2022-essential-training/finding-mass-properties?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] The weight of something is defined by the volume of it times the mass density. Now, depending on the material, obviously, we're going to have different densities. So right now, if I want to weigh this kettlebell shape here, I could come up here under Evaluate and come over here to Mass Properties. And you can see here, I get a mass of 9.99 pounds, and you're thinking, "Hey, great, I got a mass, so everything's good." Well, it's really not, right? Because we didn't define what material this is. In fact, [[SOLIDWORKS]] doesn't even know what material this is. It says, material not specified. So it just giving you some arbitrary number which doesn't really help us out at all. So I always recommend defining a material when you're designing a part. So a kettlebell is most time made out of iron. So let's go ahead and apply a material called iron to it. So come over here to Edit Material and under Steel we can troll that up, come under Iron here, and I've got the ductile iron or gray cast iron. Let's use that gray cast iron and go ahead and apply that, click on Close. And notice the appearance changed. So now it's kind of a black color. It's looking more iron-like. And over here it does say gray cast iron. So now when I run the mass properties, it should give us the exact weight of this kettlebell. So under Mass Properties here you can see now the mass is 71.92 pounds. And it's saying, hey, the density that I'm using to calculate this is 0.26 pounds per cubic inch, right? So that's important. So you got to make sure that you're using the right density to get the right weight at the end of the day.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2022-essential-training/finding-mass-properties?u=76281980&t=94)** Now, of course, the volume in the surface area shouldn't change, because the shape did not change. And each part, when you start up, it has a coordinate or default coordinate system, right at the origin. So I know if you look over here, here's our origin. That is where our default coordinate system is. And if you look at the triad down here you can see the X goes this way, the Y goes up, and the Z goes out of the page and that's what's defining that coordinate system. Now we can define our own coordinate system if you'd like. So if you come up here to reference geometry, click on coordinate system, and this of course has been at SolidWorks for a long time as well, the ability to create your own coordinate system. But what's new is this little checkbox. So check this out. So first off, if I choose something like a point, right, it's going to automatically create a coordinate system right there at that point using the default directions. Okay. Now, if I click on the check box, I have the ability to actually type in a numeric value here. So instead of 30 let's type in 40. It moves my little coordinate system out and it's not attached anymore to any other geometry. And because it's not attached to geometry, it makes your design so much more robust. Now I also have the ability to change the orientation. I can spin it around the X and the Y's. I can add all these other definitive values here by just typing in rotation values, if you need those, right. I'm not going to do that for right now. But right now let's just click on the green check box and define that system. Now once we have that coordinate system, it's even more powerful. Now you can use the axis like the Y, the X, or the Z, or these individual coordinate planes to define and draw on.
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2022-essential-training/creating-reference-coordinate-systems?u=76281980&t=96)** So what if you wanted to draw on this face right here? Let's say like the Y-Z plane click on that one right there, I can say, "Hey, let's go ahead and start a sketch." Let's draw a circle. There it is. And you can see it's it's drawing right on that plane, right? From that coordinate system. You want to create a feature? No problem, we can do that. Click OK, and there's our feature defined from that coordinate system. Okay. Now, if you want to use that coordinate system for something like a pattern, how about a circular pattern, right? My direction, I'm going to rotate around the Y of that new coordinate system. I'm going to choose a body. I'm going to choose this one right here and I can pattern around that coordinate system. So it's a really quick and easy way to define these things now. It's using numeric values. So you can define a few of these from locations in your model to define things. And if you needed to change your model later on just by moving that coordinate system all those individual features which are based upon it will automatically move. So it's a really powerful and great new addition to SolidWorks 2022.

Same thing with the end points here we can do all kinds of fun things with the shape. Once you've created it, just grabbing these points and moving these control handles around.
>
> **[3:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2022-essential-training/working-with-ellipses-and-conics?u=76281980&t=188)** And of course we can also define all those things when we create that shape originally. #### [Equation driven curve](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2022-essential-training/equation-driven-curve?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2022-essential-training/equation-driven-curve?u=76281980&t=0)** - Equation driven curves allow you to define a function that will determine the y-value based upon the x-value calculation. To get started, let's go ahead and start a sketch on the front plane and come up here under the spline tool, you can see the dropdown. We have equation driven curve right over here, and click on that. And right over here, I have the equation itself, so it's going to say y based upon x, so I have to put something in here that is related to x. So let's try x squared. Okay, and starting at 0, and let's go up here to 5. Alright, let's try that out, and right there, we can quickly see that we were able to create this curve, on the screen, that is that equation. So it's x squared is equal to y and that's the curve that you get. Now, we can also change that. We can say, y is equal to the sin of x, for instance, and right over here, notice the curve does change. Now I start getting a sin curve over here, and if I just change this value over here to maybe like 20, we get some waves of our sin curve, so you can put anything you want in this equation, all based upon x, to create or calculate the y-value. And notice these all start at the 0 point, but of course you could change that value or that position, by like adding something to it at the end. So you say y is equal to sin of x plus 10, which then would move that curve up here. Now we can also switch over here to parametric. Now parametric is basing the equation on a t-value, so let's get rid of what we have in there right now.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2022-essential-training/equation-driven-curve?u=76281980&t=94)** And we can just say t, a little t here, and a t over there, and you get a straight line, or you could say this t here is going to be squared. And see what happens there, so now you can get a curve that looks like that. And of course we don't go quite that high. So you can use this a whole bunch. So if you want to use an x and y, all based on t, or a y-value based on x, you got a lot of control over this equation driven curve inside of [[SOLIDWORKS]]. And, you know, I actually have used this, very recently, on creating a reflector for a light. And we took, an equation actually was given to me from one of my other vendors, and they said, hey, we'd like to create this in SOLIDWORKS, how can we create it? They were actually able to generate that equation out of their software, I plugged it directly in here, and we created the shape, did a revolve, and we had our reflector. So it's a really powerful tool inside of SOLIDWORKS, for working with equations and bringing 'em in from other softwares, or just creating something yourself.

Remember that if it is a powerful reference, like a face or a plane, you only need one. If you have something like a line or a point, you need two. >
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2022-essential-training/making-an-axis?u=76281980&t=95)** So choose that one there. I'm going to choose this point right there and this plane right there. So it's starting at that point on that plane. It could also be a sketch of course. And it's going to define the axis from those two inputs. So bunch of different ways you can create axes but do keep in mind though, there is those temporary axes that are hidden most of the time. They happen to be inside of most cylindrical objects already. So don't go ahead and create extra axes when you don't need to, especially when they're already there.

#### [Creating a coordinate system](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2022-essential-training/creating-a-coordinate-system?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2022-essential-training/creating-a-coordinate-system?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Each [[SOLIDWORKS]] part has a origin, as well as a default coordinate system. Notice down here is a triad, and we have an X, a Y, and a Z direction, and over here is our origin. Now, notice that origin happens to be just randomly in the middle of my part. So sometimes you might want to actually create your own coordinate system where you'd like. This is especially useful if you're trying to figure out like a moment of inertia of the part, or you want to weigh it, or find the center of mass and you want to use that default coordinate system to define where that is. It's also helpful if you ever get into CNC machining, you can define where that work offset's going to be by establishing a new coordinate system. So, I want to point out here, if I go up to Evaluate, I can come over here to Mass Properties, it's using the center of mass over here, and it's using all these values, the center of mass, from that default coordinate system, right? We only have that default system because we haven't added anymore, and same thing down here, as far as the moments of inertia and all this other data down here is all using that default coordinate system. So, if we add a new one, we can modify how that's all being calculated by using that new coordinate system. So, what I'd like to do is come up here under Features, come over here to Reference Geometry and come down to Coordinate System. Now, the point I want to use is this little point right here. I want my X-axis to go along this edge right here, and now you can see I've got these other axes lined up exactly how I'm looking for them, and everything looks good. When you're done with that, you can also choose the Y or the Z axis to define it a little further, but if you've got what you're looking for here,
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2022-essential-training/creating-a-coordinate-system?u=76281980&t=93)** you've got the X going in the direction you want, the Ys go in the right direction, as well as the Z, you are all set click Okay. And now you've got a new coordinate system. Now, you can do the same thing multiple times. We can create coordinate systems all over our part. In fact, if you wanted to create something up here like a regular coordinate system that you weren't using before, I could say, hey, I'd like to just make some basic drawing. Like starting over here I could say, I'd like to have a line going this direction and a line going that direction, and just exit out of the sketch, and then you can use those lines to define where your coordinates are going to be going. So, let's go ahead and try that one more time. I come up here to Coordinate System. I'm going to use this corner, right, as my coordinate system. My X-axis is going to go along this edge here. My Y-axis is going to go along that edge there, and then the Z's going to be going up. Click Okay, and there my next coordinate system. So, you can rename those over here as well just by clicking on it, and just renaming it right here, but you don't have to. Okay, so there's a couple different ways you can do that, and then notice over here, because I've got these two coordinate systems, if I go back to Evaluate, and come up to Mass Properties, I can now choose them right over here. I can say coordinate system one. Notice these values change. So does the center of mass. Coordinate system two, notice everything changes as well. So, you're basing that center of mass off of that new coordinate system, or you're basing the moment of inertia off that coordinate system, and if you get into CNC machining, you could say, hey, that's my work offset number one. This other place over here is going to be work offset number two, and so on. So, a lot of things you can do using these coordinate systems inside of SolidWorks.

#### [Chamfering edges](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2022-essential-training/chamfering-edges?u=76281980)

> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2022-essential-training/chamfering-edges?u=76281980&t=0)** - The Solidwork's chamfer tool works very similar to the fillet tool. So you're either going to have to surface that face which going to be hard and expensive or you're going to have to build a custom tool. #### [Applying draft](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2022-essential-training/applying-draft?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2022-essential-training/applying-draft?u=76281980&t=0)** - If you're designing a part for any type of a molding process like plastic injection molding or dye casting, you're probably going to need to add some draft. Now draft is just some angles to the outside surfaces so when the part or the mold separates, that the part comes out very easily. So to do that, we have a great tool inside of SolidWork with believe it or not, it's called draft. (chuckles) All right. So click on this one, right up here, draft. And the first question is, what angle do we want to draft at? So most of the time for plastic injection molded parts we want to have about three degrees of draft. Now you can go below that, but depending on the texture of your part, you might have some issues. So three degrees is generally a nice size draft. And of course you can have anything above that is fine. But right now, I'm going to leave nine degrees so you can see it a little easier. Now right over here is going to be the neutral plane. So that's where do you want to start that draft from. So I can choose something like the top face here, and then over here is going to be what are the faces that I want to draft. >
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2022-essential-training/applying-draft?u=76281980&t=92)** So it's going the other way. And if you look there... let's look at the top there. You can see that the draft's actually going the other way. So that's generally what you don't want to do, right? Because now it's drafting out as you go down and you'd never be able to get that out of the mold. So make sure you are drafting things the correct direction. I also want to point out while we're here, If you click over here in under the draft, we can come down and we can also choose a parting line or a step draft which, a couple of other ways you can do it as well, so if you have a parting line already defining your part which you'd probably need to add ahead of time, that's under the mold tools, then you can actually split your part from the parting line. But in general, most time you, uh, neutral plane drafts, is going to be what you're going to be probably using. If you have multiple faces, we can also use face propagation that's along the tangent or faces. In this case here, I don't have anything tangent because I have no fillets around the edges. But if I did, I could come back. So let's go ahead and try that out one time. Go over here, let's add a fillet. There's my fillet. Oh, not that one right there. Let's go back to this style here. Here's my fillet, and 0.5. All right. So there you can see, we have a fillet. Now, if I come back over here and I add some draft... Right, here's my draft. And I'm going to choose the neutral plane... and my face. I'm going to choose right here and use the manual version of this. So over here I faced propagation along tangent. I'm going to choose this one here. Oh, hold on. First, you got to choose this one up here at the top and then face it to draft that one. And then along the tangency, choose that one there,
>
> **[3:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2022-essential-training/applying-draft?u=76281980&t=184)** and notice that automatically propagates that around the edge. So a couple ways we can do that there. We also can switch over here to this draft expert which we saw slightly here. So draft analysis can be turned on. So you can see something, that, an how much draft it has. So this will give you kind of a heads up display of what's going on with your part. So you're seeing: Hey, everything's yellow has zero draft, right? Everything that's orange has some negative draft and everything that's blue has positive draft, and that will allow you, or give you a guide at least, on how to add draft to your part. Running that draft analysis tool is also another feature you can just run and you can see which of the faces hasn't had any draft added to them yet. And when you're working on like, an injection molded part, you want to make sure that all those faces coming from the parting line of your part wherever you're going to be separating it out, do have draft so they'll easily separate from the mold. So anyways, that is how you add draft to your part. It's a pretty powerful tool and there's a lot going on there. One piece of advice though is if you're going to be adding fillets, like this fillet over here, do all the drafting first and then add all the fillets second. That will definitely make your part look a little bit nicer when you get finished up.

> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2022-essential-training/direct-editing-move-face?u=76281980&t=0)** - [INstructor] The move face command is amazing. >
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2022-essential-training/direct-editing-move-face?u=76281980&t=94)** And an offset notice is making these chamfers a lot smaller and it's also making these holes smaller. > **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2022-essential-training/direct-editing-combine?u=76281980&t=0)** - The combine command is what I call a bullying operator. We can add them together, We can subtract theM or we can find what is in common. So my bodies to combine are these two. This guy and this guy. If we want to add them together, click okay. And all of a sudden we've got one body. Great, and we've used this a little bit already in the course, by combining a couple things together. And if you have two different bodies you've brought in or imported from another part, using the combine add function is really powerful. But I can also do some things that are even more interesting, like subtract. I could subtract the main bodies. That's going to be this one here, and I want to subtract. Right over here is going to be the main body. And then I'm going to subtract this. Click on okay. And now what I've done is I've removed that. I've hollowed out the center of this block from the other solid. Let's go ahead and take a look at the section view.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2022-essential-training/direct-editing-combine?u=76281980&t=95)** So from the top, right over here top, you can see inside there. I've actually taken one solid and removed it from the other solid to core out the shape. This is also really helpful if you're working with a mold or something like that and you want to take one shape and you want to cut it out of another block of material that you're going to mold. There's also some mold tools to help with this as well. But this is how you could get started. Now let's go back again to the combine feature. This time let's click on Common. So let's click on the common bodies. We're going to tick both these and then click on okay. And turn that section view off. And you can see here. This is what was common between the two bodies originally. So anything that was enclosed in one is also enclosed in the other, is what's in common and that's what we kept. So three different ways we can use the same exact tool to do a lot of work behind the scenes in [[SOLIDWORKS]]. I definitely use the combine add command probably the most because a lot of times I do bring multiple parts in and combine them together. Or I will actually have a multi-body part and then, or maybe even section my body into a few different pieces, and then to some operations on one or multiple pieces, and then use that add command to bring everything back together at the end.

#### [Review of available tool sets](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2022-essential-training/review-of-available-tool-sets?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2022-essential-training/review-of-available-tool-sets?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] [[SOLIDWORKS]] is a very powerful tool that's used in a variety of industries. Now, whether you're designing consumer [[Microsoft Products|products]], or building big weldments, maybe you're doing advanced surfacing, maybe you're an architect and you're using it for designing buildings or structural framing, there's a whole lot of things that SolidWorks can do, and we have all these tool sets that can plug into Solidworks to make it give it some more functionality that it might not have from the base package. So you have a base package here, which is your basic sketch toolbar, your features toolbar, to create your basic things like extrudes, revolves, and sweeps. Now we have a whole bunch of the tool packages we can turn on, and again, SolidWorks is a customizable environment, so you can turn these tools on or turn them off. So, to turn on different tool bars, you can come up here to any one of the tabs, right click on them, and come over here to tabs, and here are what we have. So we have sketch ink, which is a quick way to use like, a tablet to mark up some drawings. We've got the full surfaces toolbar, we've got the sheet metal toolbar, structural systems, weldments, mold tools, if you're designing, you know, plastic injection molding, you have all those tools available to us. [[Mesh]] modeling, this is something that's been added in the past couple years, allows you to bring in STL files for 3D printing and work with those, which is really handy. Data migration, working with different file types, bringing those in, direct editing is great if you're bringing in, you know, dumb solids, which are like, things like STEP files or IGES files that they don't have any feature tree, they're just bringing in an imported file and do some direct editing on it. We got the markup toolbar, you have this MBD dimensions, you have rendering, all these different things,
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2022-essential-training/review-of-available-tool-sets?u=76281980&t=93)** right, analysis preparation, so all these toolbars are available to us. So you can go ahead and just turn any of these on, and if you click on the tab, just turn them on. I can just keep going and just adding all these things in here. And I just want to kind of show you some of the more important ones that's available to you. Now this course is an essential training course, so we're not going to go into every one of these available tool sets, but I do want to point out that they're available and what is in there, so if you want to dive in to any one of these things, you can come back over here and take a look at some of the things that are available to us in all these tabs, and you can see I'm turning them all on here, so they're going to cross your screen. And normally I turn all these things off, 'cause I want to make it as simple and clean to see the SolidWorks environment without all these tool bars on, and it can overwhelm people because there's so much in SolidWorks. But don't feel overwhelmed, you can turn them off if you're not using them. But, starting over here on the left is the basic features toolbar, which are your boss, extrude, and revolves. Pretty much everything uses a sketch, so these are your basic sketch tools up here. The sketch ink one allow you to just draw on top of it or erase things, and just kind of make do markup work on there. Your surfaces, we're going to cover that in this course as well, or we already have, and surfaces allow you use them as helper surfaces to create solids or we can just create surface models. We can do some really powerful things inside of surfaces. The sheet metal toolbar we've looked at a little bit already. Of course, you're creating sheet metal parts, is a really handy toolbar, and you can switch between solids and sheet metal parts by using the convert to sheet metal tool. Structural systems and weldments kind of play together. If you're creating any type of welded structures
>
> **[3:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2022-essential-training/review-of-available-tool-sets?u=76281980&t=188)** using I-beams, or box channel, or things like that, this is a great toolbar and it really does save you a lot of time by designing and using weldments versus creating these shapes using basic extrudes and things like that. Your mold tools are very handy if you're designing injection molded parts. Now, you might not be using these tools exactly if you're just creating a molded part, but if you're creating actually the molds themselves, these tools are there to actually create the molds. But a lot of them happen to really help out when you're creating molded parts as well, like things like draft, and draft analysis, to make sure your part is set up correctly for molding. Mesh modeling, again, is when you're bringing in STL files, or one of the other formats of different subdivision surface model, or something like that, you're going to bring in and work with, you've got a whole bunch of things you can do here using these tools, and every year they're adding more and more to this tool package. Again, this is pretty, yeah, this is a little more advanced, but it allows you to, you know, to really start working with mesh bodies, and SolidWorks, in general, has not been good at working with meshes in the past, but now the tools are getting pretty powerful, and we have a lot more options. So, data immigration is bringing in imported geometry and analyzing it, and things like that, recognizing features, knitting things together, fixing faces, and so on. Your direct editing, I love direct editing, by the way. Now you can do direct editing on imported surfaces or bodies, but you can also use direct editing in your regular models, right, if you have a face and you don't want to go back through the feature tree and figure out how that face was created, you can just grab that face, move it, delete it, whatever you want to do, all in the direct editing toolbar.
>
> **[4:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2022-essential-training/review-of-available-tool-sets?u=76281980&t=280)** Now we've already covered a little bit of the direct editing in this course, but again, definitely take a look at those tools. Markup, we're also going to be covering, is a great way to just make notes on your drawings, which is an overlay on top of your existing model, which is really handy for keeping up with what's going on in your design and also collaborating with other coworkers. Evaluate, this is where all your tools like measure, and interference detection, and things like that are available, right, and evaluate, I use these all the time, my favorites are interference detection and measure, but any of these other things here are really handy and we can dive into those a little bit more as we go through the course. And I also have a couple more advanced courses that do cover some of this in more depth. MBD dimensions, so this is model-based definitions that you're going to have, or dimensions, and you know, a lot of times they're going to be using this now for, instead of creating a drawing, you're going to have these MBD dimensions in some type of a file. So instead of having to actually create a drawing, you can just pull up the model and you'll have that information available to you. Next one over here is the rendering tools. SolidWorks has a couple of different rendering packages available, and this will allow you to kind of prepare your model to get into those rendering tools. And then these are a bunch of the SolidWorks add-ins, which are going to extend SolidWorks even further, like circuit works, or photoview 360, scan to 3D, you know, motion, [[Routing]], [[Simulation]]. These are all add-ins that make SolidWorks even more powerful. And it's also, you have add-ins for like, CAM, or, you know, CNC machining, things like that. So you're going to use the basic tools of SolidWorks, and then just extend them a little further
>
> **[6:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2022-essential-training/review-of-available-tool-sets?u=76281980&t=372)** using one of these packages here. And the last one here is the analysis preparation. Sometimes when you're working and preparing to do an FEA analysis using SolidWorks simulation, you need to simplify it, right, and get rid of extra things that are going to cause problems for the analysis, and that's when you're going to jump into this analysis toolbar. So anyway, that's a long, (laughs) long winded overview of some of these tools. Obviously we don't have the ability to get into every single one of these things, but I do want to point out that they're there, and we have courses on almost every one of these things, or a lot of them, in the library, so if there's something you're interested in, definitely take a look at that course in the library.

#### [Mating parts together in an assembly](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2022-essential-training/mating-parts-together-in-an-assembly?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2022-essential-training/mating-parts-together-in-an-assembly?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Let's talk about mates inside of [[SOLIDWORKS]]. Now that I've got my assembly started, I've got individual parts inside of my assembly, I can grab and I can move these components around on my screen by left-clicking on 'em and dragging 'em around. I can rotate them by right mouse clicking on 'em and rotating 'em around. Now we need to actually connect them together using mates. So the best way to mate something together is to actually say what you would like to connect together out loud. It might sound a little funny to do but if you actually say it out loud, it really makes it easy to figure out what things we need to click and mate together. So before we do that, let's click on the Mate toolbar right up here. So click on Mate, and notice we've got all these standard mates. We get Coincident, Parallel, Perpendicular, Tangent, Concentric, and so on. Now SolidWorks are actually figure this out for us. If we choose nothing over here in this window, if we just start selecting faces on our parts, SolidWorks are generally come up with a suggested mate type. Now we can change that, but let me give you an example of how it works. But we have to add generally three mates to each individual part to fully define where it is in space.

#### [Working with subassemblies](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2022-essential-training/working-with-subassemblies?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2022-essential-training/working-with-subassemblies?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Subassemblies are assemblies of other assemblies. Now right here on my screen I've got a subassembly that's already been created. It's actually just called an assembly at this point. And what we're going to do is we're going to create an assembly of this assembly. So come up here under File. Come down here to Make Assembly of, or From Assembly. Click on that. It's automatically going to be selected over here 'cause I only have one thing open. Click on Okay. And now that subassembly shows up into my top level assembly. If you want to make a copy of this entire assembly, we can hold down Control and drag out another copy right over here. In fact, we can drag out as many copies as we'd like. If I grab it here, click on this one here, hold down Control, just drag out that assembly, there you have it. So now I have three of the individual subassembly. Now, if you twirl down any one of these, you can see the components that happen to make that up. If you hold down Control and drag out a copy of any one of these, it's only going to bring the part itself, right? So now it's just the part, not the entire assembly. So if you do want to make a copy of the entire subassembly, drag it over here from the list over here. Okay, now one thing that's really cool about subassemblies is if you make a modification to that subassembly, it'll automatically propagate through to your top level. So let's go ahead and spin this around, and you can see here we've got a bunch of these parts and I'm going to put a washer that goes around this component here. So let's go ahead and open up this subassembly, and then right over here on the right hand side of the screen, notice there's the File Explorer. And right up there at the top you can see I'm actually in the Exercise Files
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2022-essential-training/working-with-subassemblies?u=76281980&t=93)** on the desktop. This is just a quick way to get access to these files. And the part I'm looking for here is this 11.4.0.2 washer. And let's go ahead and just drag that into this subassembly. All right, now we're going to mate that together, so I'm going to choose the outside of this pin and the inside of the washer, and then the side of this face here and this face here. Those are going to slide together, click Okay, and now we're done with the mating. Now, if I jump back over into that top level assembly, you can see that washer's now been added to all three of the subassemblies. So that's going to save us a lot of work, right? Instead of having to add those mates and bring that component in three times, you add only one time to the subassembly and it automatically shows up here. Now notice this one up here did not get that washer because it is not a subassembly. This is just one part, so it's not related. A good way to think about subassemblies is in real life, how would you assemble these items? And that's exactly how you should do it in a computer. If you have five components, they need to be put together first and then added to your top level assembly, and you're going to do that multiple times, definitely build an assembly of those five components first and then bring it in multiple times. That way you can always go back and modify that subassembly and all that work will automatically propagate through. This is how you can work with subassemblies. It's great and powerful tool inside of [[SOLIDWORKS]]. Make sure you're doing it anytime you're repeating an assembly more than one time.

#### [Using the Q key for quick references](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2022-essential-training/using-the-q-key-for-quick-references?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2022-essential-training/using-the-q-key-for-quick-references?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Narrator] One of the most powerful types of mates is a plane to plane mate. And it's pretty easy to create. And in fact, each part we have in this assembly for instance, if you look inside there, you twirl it down you can see that there's those three fundamental planes that you start with on pretty much every single part. And assemblies have the same thing, they have that right plane, right there, here's your top plane and your front plane, right? So those are there and they're available. And I can select them over here from the tree here. If I hold down control I can select a few different planes from different parts if I wanted to. So in this case here, I can say, hey, how about the, you know, maybe the right plane of this part here, come down here to this part here and the right plane on that part. And that might be one way we want to do that, right? Or you could say like, hey, here's the the right plane of the assembly, and then come down here to the right plane of this part here. I could say, hey, let's make those mirrored, or I can specify a certain distance away from one of those things. The problem is, is it's a little bit clunky having to go into the feature managers, you know, find where the part is, you know, kind of come down here and figure where these things are. And there's got to be a better way. Well, guess what? Now there is, there's this little key on your keyboard which is the Q key. So if you click on a part and hit Q, it automatically brings up all the relevant planes right there in context. Let's try it again. If I go over on this side of over here I can hit the Q on my keyboard. And again, it's going to bring up the relevant planes of that part right there. And then as soon as I click away, they just go away and I don't have to show them
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2022-essential-training/using-the-q-key-for-quick-references?u=76281980&t=93)** and then go back and hide them, do anything else like that. So it really makes it an easy way to just grab those relevant planes really quickly. So let's try it out. If I choose a mate, I'm going to go ahead and do my mate selection. I'm going to hover over here, I'm going to hit Q, I'm going to say, Hey, how about that edge, sorry, that plane right there, which is the right plane. That part here. And I'd say, let's go ahead and do the same thing over here. I'm going to choose this plane right there. I say, eh, I don't want that, I want them to be just a distance apart. So let's type in like five inches and let's change it from actually, you know what, I think I chose the wrong plane. Let's get rid of that plane here, let's delete it. I want the right plane, which is there, click okay, and bam, just that quickly we're able to create that mate. So it just allows us to kind of grab those planes inside of a part really quickly. Even if you don't which part it is in the design tree, just hover over it. Again, hit the Q, it brings it up and they go away as soon as you're done. Anyways, so that's the new Q key for quick selection of planes.

#### [Mating parts by aligning planes](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2022-essential-training/mating-parts-by-aligning-planes?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2022-essential-training/mating-parts-by-aligning-planes?u=76281980&t=0)** - When you're mating components together inside of [[SOLIDWORKS]], there is a hierarchy, and the more powerful of an object you choose to mate together, the more powerful your mate will be between the objects. Now, in this part here, notice there are no flat faces on the sides. There's one here at the bottom. So if I were to mate, like the bottom here to this part over here, that would work pretty good, but I don't really have any other way to align this part, except the planes that are fundamentally there. So right over here in that part, if I click on the part, you can see I've got my front plane, I've got the top plane and I've got the right plane. So instead of choosing the bottom face, I could actually choose the top plane. Now, what's really amazing and powerful about that is if I completely delete the part or completely modify the part, that fundamental plane, the top plane will still be there. That right plane, that top plane, the front plane, all those things are fundamentally there before we created anything else. So if you use those fundamental planes to mate components together, you've created the strongest mate possible. If you use a point, it's very easy to delete that point. If you add a radius, or a filler or something like that, or just modify the part, if that point goes away, guess what? That mate also goes away. Same thing with a line. A line is easy to get rid of. It's more powerful than a point, but a line's also easy to get rid of. A plane or a face of a part is a little bit more powerful, but still nowhere close as powerful as one of the fundamental planes. So in this video, I want to teach you about using those fundamental planes to make components together.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2022-essential-training/mating-parts-by-aligning-planes?u=76281980&t=93)** So, looking at this part over here, let's go ahead and take a look at what fundamental planes are in there. So I've got the front plane, I've got the top plane and I've got the right plane. Alright, so what I can do here is I can click on "Mate", and I can go ahead and choose the top surface here, which is not as powerful as the plane. But, over here on the part itself, that I want to mate together, I can come down here and I can say, Hey, this top plane, I can mate those two things together. Click on the green check-mark. And there you have it. Alright. The next thing I want to do is I want to choose those planes. Now notice how I went through and looked for the planes over here and kind of highlighted them. The other thing I can do is come over here to the View or Visibility window and I can choose to "Show Planes". So if I turn on the planes here, I can see them and then, I can easily grab those planes for mating. And if you don't see those planes, you can always come over here and make sure that you click on it with a left mouse-click and make sure you're showing or hiding it. So make sure you're showing the ones you want to use. And sometimes you want to hide the ones that are not going to be applicable to what we're doing. So come up here to mate. I'm going to choose this plane here to that plane there. They slide together, looks good. And this plane right over here, notice as I highlight over it, it just highlights. And, I can choose that plane. There it is. And just that easily, I was able to mate those components together. When you're done looking at those planes, just go ahead and turn them off right over here. And now I have almost an unbreakable mate. The only mate I could screw up here would be the top surface here,
>
> **[3:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2022-essential-training/mating-parts-by-aligning-planes?u=76281980&t=185)** because I could delete that top surface or I could do something to it. But everything else is super solid, because it is using those planes that we can't destroy. So if you do decide to mate components together, try to choose the most powerful entities you can to mate. The best are the planes, because they're always there. And it's also the easiest for SolidWorks to figure out because it's not based upon any geometry that's actually in the model. It's just that fundamental plane. The worst is a point. The second worst is a line. Third worst is a face. And then, planes are much better than that. Of course, the fundamental planes are the absolute best. So definitely try that out when you're working on your next assembly model.

#### [Magnetic Mates](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2022-essential-training/magnetic-mates?u=76281980)

> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2022-essential-training/magnetic-mates?u=76281980&t=0)** - Magnetic mates are a really quick and easy way to mate together individual components. If I choose a different configuration which is like a zero, click okay. Oh, they'll spin that thing around. Or if I click on that and I say, instead of that I want 120 degrees, click okay. And notice my entire design has changed because I'm choosing a different configuration but these little magnetic mates are still there. Let's go ahead and grab a couple more components here. This guy over here, let's snap them right over here. Do the same thing over here. Grab this guy over here, slide him over. And notice as we get close, We get that little line that connects those components together. So just that easily I can combine as many of these little pieces together very quickly. Now, if you don't like one of those mates, grab that component and start dragging it around. Of course I can move things around here.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2022-essential-training/magnetic-mates?u=76281980&t=94)** Or if you click on the component here, I can also look at the mates right over here. And here are my coincident mates ahead of time. And here's all my magnetic mates. And if you don't like one of those, just click on it, hit delete and that goes away. Now I can grab that component, slide it around. And of course I can mate it to somewhere else. So that's my first example for magnetic mates. Now I'm going to switch over to my second example which is right over here. This is some PVC pipe components that I've created. I've just got a basic pipe section here as well as a 90 degree segment. And if you wanted to continue on with this design you can just Control-drag out another copy of a component. Notice that one there. And then just go ahead and grab this component. Put it up here to the top. Let go. If I need another pipe, click on that pipe, drag out another component. Oh, not that one. Hold on. Hold on. Control-Grab a component out. There it is. And then as we get close here, it'll snap together. Now sometimes it can get a little wily, If you start dragging things it starts connecting and trying to connect everywhere it can see another connection point. So you do have to keep that in mind but let's try it one more time. Control-drag out a component, bring it over here. And then as we get close, it's going to snap together. And just that quickly we're able to connect components together. So these magnetic mates work really really well for things like this, like piping, right? If you're using the same component over and over again you can spend the time to create those magnetic mates that are associated with that one part. And then anytime you're using it with other components that have magnetic mates those things are going to snap together. And it's a really quick way to attach your components without using traditional mates inside of [[SOLIDWORKS]].

In fact, a nice example of this is a company that I know makes [[Hardware]] or fasteners like bolts, and they make all kinds of different sizes of bolts and all kinds of different lengths of those bolts. But a bolt is basically the same shape. A socket cap screw is always pretty much the same shape. It's just bigger or smaller or longer or shorter. So we can configure that in a design table to have literally thousands of different sizes of bolts all in one table, and you can easily switch between any one of them at any time.

#### [Choosing the correct projection angle](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2022-essential-training/choosing-the-correct-projection-angle?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2022-essential-training/choosing-the-correct-projection-angle?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] When working with [[SOLIDWORKS]] drawings, we need to make sure we're choosing the correct projection style. Now, what we're looking here at the screen is what's called a third angle projection. Now, if you're in the US, most of the time we do use a third angle projection style, but if you're pretty much anywhere else in the world, they use the first angle projection. So it's a very subtle difference between the two, but if you're looking at a drawing expecting it to be a certain way, it's very easy to get confused if you're looking at a first angle projection and you're expecting a third angle projection. Well, let me show you what a third angle projection is and then we'll compare that to a first angle projection, and you can see why it's such a common mistake. So the way I was taught this originally was if you had a big mixing bowl, and you put your part in the bottom of the mixing bowl. And you took that part and you kind of slid it up the side of the bowl, that the view you'd get over here on the side would be this view here. Same thing if you were to take that same part and create another projection. So I'm going to go over here to Drawing, Projected View and take that same part and slid it up the bowl on this side, you'd get that projection. So let's go ahead and take a look at the part itself. So click on the part, open the part and let's go ahead and just make sure we're looking at the part correctly. So here's our part. And you can see here, I've got this front view here. And then if I flip over to the side, I've got this one with this little hexagon, and I got a triangle at the top. All right, so that's what the part looks like in 3D. Now let's head back over to the drawing. And so here's that front view. Here's the side view and the top view. This is a third angle projection, right? And a lot of times drawings actually will say
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2022-essential-training/choosing-the-correct-projection-angle?u=76281980&t=93)** which style of projection they're using down here in the block. This title block doesn't happen to have that. So you do want to make sure you're aware of the system that they are using. So let's go ahead and switch over to first angle projection, and I'll show you how the drawing changes. But before we do that, let's go ahead and add a couple extra projections. So over here I'm going to click... oop, not that one. Let's start from that view there, Projected View. And here's the view here with nothing on it and then same thing down here. One more time, projection view, a blank view. Okay, so those are all the different views we have of our part in third angle. Now right-click anywhere in the gray area. Come over here to Properties. If you don't see Properties, click over here on the drop down, grab Properties. And notice it's third angle projection. If I switch over to first angle projection, apply the changes, notice everything changes, right? So now instead of having that hexagon here, it's over here on this side. Instead of having the triangle here at the top, it's now here at the bottom, so it's exactly the same part, it's just how we're looking at it. And with first angle projection, instead of having a mixing bowl with your part in the bottom, it's a mixing bowl turned upside down, and you have that part. Now you're rolling that part down the different faces. And what you're seeing is the first angle projection. So just keep in mind how that is done and just be aware that different places in the world use the different projection styles. And so if you're seeing a drawing and it doesn't totally make sense, you're probably looking at a first angle of projection versus a third angle or a third angle when you're expecting a first angle.

> **[0:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2022-essential-training/creating-general-dimension-notations?u=76281980&t=1)** - In this chapter, we're going to be learning about drawing dimensions. We'll cover the baseline dimensions, chain dimensions, ordinate dimensions, plus so much more. Let's go ahead and dive in. General dimensions can be added to your part or your drawing view inside of [[SOLIDWORKS]], pretty straightforward and easily, by clicking on the annotation toolbar and coming over here to smart dimension. Now directly below smart dimension here are a whole bunch of other ways we can add dimensions. But smart dimension generally picks the best dimension possible, instead of having to choose individual, like horizontal or vertical dimensions and so on, the smart dimension will do most of that for us. So go ahead and choose that one there. Now we have to choose which face of our part is our datum, which one is going to start all of our dimensions. So in this case here, I could choose either this face here, or this one over here. Now this one over here makes more sense to me, so I'm going to start there, and I'm going to start dimensioning to certain things. So if I choose like, this first hole, I've got a dimension between the two. So click the face you want to start from and then choose the face you want to end to. Once we got the center marks in there we can continue adding more dimensions. Now I always want to start from this face, right? Some people will try to go and add a dimension like from here to here.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2022-essential-training/creating-general-dimension-notations?u=76281980&t=93)** Can you do that? Yes, but it's not a good practice, right? Because you're now pulling a dimension from a different side of the part. You're not using the main datum target. So you have to have a pretty reason why you would do this a better way to do it would be delete that and then dimension directly from this face over here. Okay. So what we call this dimensioning scheme is a baseline dimensioning scheme. You keep coming from the same baseline and adding more and more dimensions across your part, and they start stacking up like you might see right here. And again, jump back over in that, in that tool always go back to that same exact baseline. Another way though, you could dimension things is what we call chain dimensioning. So instead of dimensioning like that I can delete these guys here. I could say, well, this one is 1.25. And then from that hole to the next hole I can add a dimension there. And then from that hole to the next hole I can add a dimension there. The problem with this style of dimensioning though is if there's a tolerance here. So if this is a plus or minus five thousandths tolerance for this first hole, and then this is also a five thousandths that that hole actually can be 10 thousandths, plus or minus 10 thousandths from this baseline. And down here, we get another stack of five thousandths plus or minus five thousandths. And so this hole can be plus or minus 15 thousandths from this face over here. Whereas if you added a dimension that was from this face here over to this hole here then that one is constrained to plus or minus five thousandths from this edge. So that's important to understand the difference between chain dimensioning and baseline dimensioning. Now, another thing we could do is a
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2022-essential-training/creating-general-dimension-notations?u=76281980&t=189)** combination of the two. So let's go ahead and delete both of these. And in this case here, I'm going to delete these and I'm going to say, Hey, these first holes are 0.3 from the edge, and where they start. it's probably not that critical. So instead of a three place decimal I can switch over here to maybe like a two place decimal. Oh, there it is two places. So I can loosen that up a little bit more, right? So it's a little bit easier to hit that dimension. Then from there, I can say, hey, from there I want to make a dimension to the next hole. And then from there I might want to make a dimension to the next hole from there. So everything's based upon that first set of holes. And now we're creating baseline dimensions from that first set of holes, right? And it really depends on your design. What's important. In this case here maybe it's the hole to hole dimensions that are more important than from this edge. So in that case here, you could say, well, you know that's really not that important here, so I'd rather prefer to dimension from this hole to that hole. That's my critical dimension stack right there, five inches. And this one here doesn't make any sense. Let's get rid of that one. And that one is more important between this hole and that hole, and that is using the baseline dimensioning from this hole here. So again, just look at your design as a whole and figure out where, and the best way to add in those dimensions. All right, let's do a few more right over here. If I want to make an angle, choose this face here choose this face over here and add the dimension that way. Now, if we want to add some additional dimensions we might need a couple extra views. Let's go ahead and add a couple views here. Projected view. I'm going to project right up here to the top and then maybe one over here to the side. Okay. Now when I'm adding more dimensions here,
>
> **[4:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2022-essential-training/creating-general-dimension-notations?u=76281980&t=283)** I don't want to choose a different datum surface, right? I want to use that same edge, which is this one here. I want to use that same edge right up here at the top. Right? So when I dimension from here to here, I always want to choose that same surface, right? Same thing, if you're looking at the view on this other side here, we want to make sure we're choosing the same datum target. So up here, if I'm dimensioning from here to the hole, what I'm using is that front face. So over here, I would want to dimension everything from the same front face here to something else on the drawing. I only have that same hole so I can't really do anything there, but you always want to use that same face in any different views you're using. We have the option of adding a hole callout and right up here is the hole callout and right over here, I can choose a hole. And if I click on that hole, it automatically gives me all this information as far as the size of the drill bit, and how deep I want to drill, and what type of tapped hole, and the countersink that I'm adding in there, and the degrees. All that information, all with one click, is pretty handy. So that's how you add your basic dimensions to a drawing inside of Solidworks. There's a bunch of tools up here and we're going to be covering a lot of 'em as we go through the course, but that's your basic dimension scheme that you might use when you're starting out with your first few drawings.

#### [Creating ordinate dimensions](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2022-essential-training/creating-ordinate-dimensions?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2022-essential-training/creating-ordinate-dimensions?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Hey guys, let's learn about ordinate dimensions. But otherwise, we probably don't want to have a ton of dimensions on the assembly drawings. So anyways, that is how you bring in an assembly into a drawing and get a couple dimensions on there, explode out the views, and get ready to start adding in some build materials as well as some balloons to define which parts are in your assembly.
