You can define how you want a group together those parts, you can keep missing rows and items, you got a bunch of options here, we can even change the increment and what we start at for the numbering. We can also change the layer that the table is on, but for right now let's just go ahead and use the default values, click on OK, and that should bring in this table. Okay, we need to make a little space, so I'm going to grab this view here, slide that over and just make a little space on our drawing so we can include our table. All right, that's looking a little better, and now we've got space for this table. Now, once I have the table, if I [[Zoom]] in here, you can see that nothing's really organized very well, and I generally like to have things in alphabetical or numeric order. So we can do a little bit of a sort here, so if you go to the top, right-click on that column, and I can come down here to Sort and I can sort it by part number ascending or descending, you can do a multi sort, so you can sort by another column if you wanted to, and that's pretty much all you need to do. Click on that, and here we go. So it sorts 'em, but notice it didn't sort them
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2022-essential-training/including-a-bill-of-materials?u=76281980&t=95)** the right way. It starts with P1, then it goes to 10, 11, 12, and then you got P2 down here. So it's doing them by blocks of two, it's only sorting by the number, so it's not perfect, but it's pretty easy then to just grab a row and just bring it up here, and notice down here at the bottom, we've got the three through nine, so it does group 'em together, makes this handy, and it really depends on how you label your part. So this labeling, or the part number scheme that you used here is probably not the best one, 'cause it doesn't sort well. but it's pretty easy to get this looking pretty good. So right there, you can see, we pretty much are sorted. There it is, so a couple small modifications here, So all the parts are sorted now, and then notice the descriptions are missing for most of these items. So this first item here does have a description, the [[Representational State Transfer (REST)|rest]] of 'em do not, also sometimes you might want to include a revision. So if you want to add a column, I can right-click here, I can say Insert and I can add a column to the right or left, I'm going to put one to the right, and then you can link that to a custom property or unit of measure, an equation, you got all these different things here if you want to add that column. I'm going to click on CUSTOM PROPERTY, and the property I want to link to is a revision. Now you have to have these custom properties in your parts. So if these are not available in your part then you definitely will not have the access to grab them, but in this case, I do have revision, and I'm going to go ahead and grab that there. I can squeeze that column a little bit and we're looking pretty good. Now to continue on, what I'd like to do is probably go into all these individual parts here and add their correct revision, and add some kind of meaningful description to each one of those so you actually know what parts are where,
>
> **[3:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2022-essential-training/including-a-bill-of-materials?u=76281980&t=188)** and a good description will really help that out. So to open up any one of these parts, you can click on them, right-click on that row and say Open Part, that part will open up, click on No here, and then come up here to the custom properties, and this is where you'd fill it out. So right here, notice only Price is in there, you can come down here and add a description as well as your revision. And over here, I'm just going to call this one Rod, and revision A and then click OK. So that information's been added to the custom properties. Now, if I head back over here to my sheet, notice that Rod has been added to my description as well as my revision, and I should be all set. Of course you can add a whole bunch more custom properties, but in this example, I just want to show you that you can quickly add those custom properties, and they will show up in the Bill of Materials as well as when you make a drawing of that part, it will also show up in the description as well as the revision for that part.

#### [Detailing mode](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2022-essential-training/detailing-mode?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2022-essential-training/detailing-mode?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Whenever you open a drawing in [[SOLIDWORKS]]. There's several different modes you can work in. So the most basic of these modes is called Resolve, right? And what that's going to do, is it's going to open the drawing and then whatever's in that drawing whether it's an assembly or individual parts it's going to go look on your file system for where those parts are supposed to be and open them up. And if there's a change it's going to take that information and it's going to resolve it and bring it into who the latest drawing. So we have all the information you need. Now, the next mode below that is Lightweight and it's pretty much going to do the same thing. It's going to look at the file system, find all the parts of an assemblies, and it's going to load them in, but it's going to load them in lightweight and it's not going to bring all the information. It's just going to find it and kind of reference it. But then if you want to actually work on that component you do need to resolve it. But what's cool is if you have like, you know, 100 components in your design or your assembly and you're opening, them up all Lightweight you can then resolve just like one or two of them work on those, and it's not going to affect everything but the assembly's going to spin around and you can work with it a lot easier because everything else has been loaded Lightweight. Now, noticing both Lightweight and Resolve mode. You have the option of like loading hidden components. You can use a speed pack, which will speed up. You know, if you want to bring like a circuit board it has like a thousand components on it. You don't really care where those components are. You're not going to work on the components. You just want to use it as a reference. You can create a speed pack for that. So a couple options there samely like large assembly settings. You can change things in SolidWorks. So when you open a really big assembly it applies certain things. It makes it go faster. So you can turn these things on or off in Lightweight to Resolve mode, right? So you can, you know, flip the difference between the two of them. But if you go over here to Detailing mode that goes away. So, Detailing mode is almost like a PDF, right?
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2022-essential-training/detailing-mode?u=76281980&t=96)** It's opening just the drawing. It's not going to look at the file system at all, right? And what is cool about that? It is you can take that drawing and you can send it to somebody else you're working with. They can do some work on it. They can send it back to you. You can do whatever you want with this drawing but nothing's linked to the individual parts yet, you have to kind of relink it. So when you go back and resolve that drawing again it's going to go look and say, 'Hey, you know, there's some changes that happen in the Detailing mode. Let's go ahead and apply those changes back to the final drawing.' So that's pretty much what Detailing mode is used for is just quick and easy way to open a big drawing with a bunch of components on it without having to load them all up. And you could have again, move it around but to different people. And you only have to transfer just that drawing you normally if you're going to send a drawing to somebody, if you just send the drawing it's going to try to open up all those files. It's not going to find them. It's going to say, 'Hey I can't find the parts assemblies it's a mess.' So then you have to do like a pack and go, you like pack up all those parts assemblies send it with the drawing and then they can open it and they have to send all those things back. You know, that's a lot of work, right? So it's not a lot easier just to send just the drawing. And that's what Detailing mode allows us to do. So right over here, let's go ahead and open up that drawing. You can see here at the top of the screen when you're in Detailing mode it's giving you heads up, says, 'Hey, you're in this mode' and you can do things like viewing, printing, and exporting, you know, from this mode, you know? And you can also look at the limitations here. It's going to give you a little heads up of what you can do with this mode. And, you know, you can do things like adding dimensions and things like that. I mean, like, you know, a lot of things are available in this mode that you can do.
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2022-essential-training/detailing-mode?u=76281980&t=189)** So like add a dimension, sure, right? And go up here and we can say, save, right? Save the file. And there you go, okay? And when you're done, you just exit out of the file, right? And if I jump back in and try to open it again and notice over here I'm opening it just from the recent documents. If I look in this little arrow here it gives me option to jump into Lightweight, Detailing. So the same things are available here, right? So, if I were to go to Resolved and say open now it's going to bring in those changes automatically link everything back together. And here's to my file. So it's not perfect, you know? But it does make things a little bit easier to work with. They're great if you're just going to like print a document and notice over here, this is showing up as not connected. So you might have to rebuild it for this to come in, and actually happen correctly. But it's definitely like if you just want to print a big document, you want to go, you know, bring and load everything in. This is definitely the way to do it or if somebody sends you a document or a drawing like this and they forget to attach all the other parts, you know, in the assembly, you just want to see what's going on in the drawing Detailing mode is definitely a great way to do that. So definitely keep in mind Detailing mode when you're working with larger assemblies, with a lot of components and you just wan to do something quick like printing or reviewing the documents. That's the mode for you.
